<div id="review_65540956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65540956" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/612774-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442497190p2/612774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65540956?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/612774-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780771051531" href="/book/show/6309920-the-kindly-ones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=wwii-holocaust">wwii-holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=1001-list">1001-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=german-ic-y">german-ic-y</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=500-plus-pages">500-plus-pages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=recommended-novels">recommended-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/612774-sarah?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65540956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2405359473276791688">Reading “The Kindly Ones” is like roaming around a dilapidated mansion – it begs you to explore; it is both fascinating and repulsive. The book is very ambitious, and it’s a pleasure to read literature that takes on a serious if uncomfortable subject, and literature that takes itself seriously. I would have given this five stars, and I do find it largely successful, but there are some snags in the subplot that don’t quite work.<br/><br/>The overarching historical plot works well. The protagonist, Maximili</span>
  <span id="freeText2405359473276791688" style="display:none">Reading “The Kindly Ones” is like roaming around a dilapidated mansion – it begs you to explore; it is both fascinating and repulsive. The book is very ambitious, and it’s a pleasure to read literature that takes on a serious if uncomfortable subject, and literature that takes itself seriously. I would have given this five stars, and I do find it largely successful, but there are some snags in the subplot that don’t quite work.<br /><br />The overarching historical plot works well. The protagonist, Maximilien Aue, visits the WWII hot spots: Babi Yar, Stalingrad, Auschwitz, and Berlin as it falls. I found the perspective gripping. And rather than finding his interaction with actual notorious Nazis ridiculous - Himmler, Speer, Eichmann, Höss, Mengele, Hitler and Bormann, his mustache brush - I found the approach better than fictionalizing the bigwigs with aliases we’d have to guess at. Mostly, I appreciated the point of view – I’ve read other WWII novels but few if any in which we’re escorted through by a perpetrator of the Holocaust. It’s a privileged if disgusting view. While there is obscene crime in the book, no need to get all righteous about it and refuse to read on. (If your stomach is weak, don’t even try this book.) The protagonist would tell you that everything that happened in WWII was caused by human beings, not monsters or genetic mutants. Aue himself doesn’t seem designed to represent some horrid beast. For the most part, he appears to be a cultivated, educated but otherwise ordinary man sucked into the infernal machine.<br /><br />And yet – and here comes my problem with the subplot – he’s not some ordinary man. He is one fucked-up cookie with some heavy issues, mostly being obsessively in love with his twin sister, with whom he longs to be one. And he unfairly despises his French mother, whom he feels betrayed his German father, thus setting up psychologically Aue’s allegiance to the Fatherland. And he is not just compelled to murder as a consequence of war, but he’s also an out-and-out murderer. Aue would also have preferred to be a woman. No sin there, but definitely not some ordinary guy tricked into doing the devil’s business. His sexual perversions don’t stop at desiring his sister. He also gets it on with a specially whittled tree branch at the tail-end of an excessive and prolonged masturbatory orgy. The fantasies! Ugh. I was both surprised and not surprised that “The Kindly Ones” won the “Bad Sex Award” for fiction in 2009. Not for sex that takes place – rather a wet dream. Oy.<br /><br />At the end, Aue is pursued both by oncoming Russians but also by “the kindly ones,” aka the Furies, for his personal crimes. I both enjoyed this mix of main plot/subplot and found it distracting. And in all cases, Aue knows he is guilty and yet doesn’t seem to regret anything, for various reasons.<br /><br />On the downside, the author does tend to go off on tangents that can be worse than tedious – they’re sometimes hard to follow. Many deal with bureaucratic issues, but for me the most exasperating was the long monologue on Caucasian linguistics by Aue’s friend Voss. I suppose the point about the difficulty and/or futility of being able to identify a language’s/people’s true origins was clear, still the author really begged the reader’s patience. I can only imagine that such passages are intended to play up the contrast of how tedium exists alongside horror. Nevertheless, whereas I would have given up on other books, this was too interesting to put down. Once the dull spots were slogged through, it was easy to get caught up again.<br /><br />My favorite parts of the book were the hallucinatory segments. When Aue’s arm frees itself from his body of its own accord and goes off shooting wildly, disconnected from him. When he is shot in Stalingrad and suddenly he’s swimming the Volga, then floating about in a dirigible with a mad scientist type. When the masturbatory episode exhausts itself and Aue imagines a dead figure in the snow.<br /><br />Finally, I liked the figure of Helene. It was important to me that she was part of the book. The scene where Aue tells her what the deal out east really is brings it home for me. She says the German people will have to pay for their crimes, and Aue responds, yes, if they lose, their enemies will be pitiless. But Helene says even if the Germans win they are going to pay. So true! I found her a sobering anchor-point in this sprawling book. Unfortunately, Helene has to be abandoned, because Aue is guilty and condemned and although human, he must be divorced from human comfort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2405359473276791688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_179512014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179512014" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179512014?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061353468" href="/book/show/6581343-the-kindly-ones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179512014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9120388384961298099">You're given a free trip to Europe, time-travelling back to world war two. Your vehicle is one of the two characters you are free to choose from: one, a Jew who hid for many months, was caught by the Germans, placed in a concentration camp, then died before the war was over; the other a Nazi officer who had been everywhere, practically met everyone of consequence in the German military, and survived the war. You will be like the character's shadow. You will be with him wherever he goes, 24/7. Yo</span>
  <span id="freeText9120388384961298099" style="display:none">You're given a free trip to Europe, time-travelling back to world war two. Your vehicle is one of the two characters you are free to choose from: one, a Jew who hid for many months, was caught by the Germans, placed in a concentration camp, then died before the war was over; the other a Nazi officer who had been everywhere, practically met everyone of consequence in the German military, and survived the war. You will be like the character's shadow. You will be with him wherever he goes, 24/7. You will see, hear and experience everything within the ambit of his senses. You will suffer no harm, but neither can you change anything that had happened. <br /><br />Which character will you choose to hitch a ride with? <br /><br />Read this book and you'll be the shadow of Sturmbannfuhrer Maximilian Aue (later promoted to Obersturmbannfuhrer, the rank equivalent to the Lieutenent-Colonel of the American army), an officer in the SS in an almost perpetual state of throwing up and diarrhea. He is a lawyer, an expert in constitutional law, but prefers to call himself a jurist, thus "Dr. Aue," before the war. He is also gay, yet has an infantile, obsessive and unbreakable incestuous bond with is only sibling, his twin sister. With him, you'll see Europe at war, criss-crossing Germany, Poland, France, Russia, etc. He was even there at Stalingrad, surviving the siege a false hero. Through this guy you'll see the glory of Nazi Germany at its mightiest and the horrors of the concentration camps it created. You'll have close contacts with the architects of the Holocaust, guys like Heinrich Himmler, Adolf Eichmann and even Hitler himself whose nose Aue manages to bite, and hear them discuss philosophy, music and their inmates' diet. The common holocaust literature is written from the Jews' point of view. Here, you'll meet the SUFFERING German officers and soldiers, driven to savagery, brutality and, sometimes, insanity because they are forced to do things "for Germany" which deep inside their hearts they know are morally reprehensible. Like they are forced to gas their own parents, children or friends, they cannot disobey, these people had become Germany's enemies, so they instead vent their anger upon these unfortunate, innocent victims, blaming them FOR BEING THERE. <br /><br />I could have easily disliked this book because of the novel written in it. The novel's length alone makes it unattractive to one like me whose TBR list grows longer everytime I visit a bookstore (which is everyday). The paragraphs are long; Aue sounds ridiculous in his supposedly dramatic moments; those improbable coincidences in an otherwise historically-accurate events; unmemorable prose, pretentious declamations and, again, Aue's fountains of shit and vomit which you can practically smell in every page. <br /><br />But who cares? I had gone into an adventure, went to hell and now I'm back. Which novel you've read can boast of accomplishing something like this for its readers? Thus, four stars and to hell with its critics!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9120388384961298099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

You don't need to know more than that to determine if this book is for you. Burroughs homage. The historical details are startling, as expected. Length is a positive virtue in historical novels in some instances. The </span>
  <span id="freeText17002440243013834097" style="display:none">I could try to compose a lengthy review, but the essential points are in the product description. Burroughs homage. The historical details are startling, as expected. Length is a positive virtue in historical novels in some instances. In essence, the author was attempting, I think, to desensitize the reader, and the accumulation of atrocities was astounding. It has the major advantage over Chuck Palahniuk of being of historical interest, instead of an excessive display of bodily functions dissected and put on display for shock value.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133665608?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2012">r-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=disturbation">disturbation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133665608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10111672564209322196">Madness. Despicably disgustingly amazingly crafted madness. The ability of authors to write out these scenarios, diving into and drowning in the minds of the most horrific human beings imaginable, without completely losing their minds astounds me sometimes. <br/><br/>Maximilian Aue is just a byproduct of this whole history, if you can believe it. He starts out with horrific tendencies, to be sure <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[: incest from an extremely young age, coprophilia, murderous inclinations <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And </span>
  <span id="freeText10111672564209322196" style="display:none">Madness. Despicably disgustingly amazingly crafted madness. The ability of authors to write out these scenarios, diving into and drowning in the minds of the most horrific human beings imaginable, without completely losing their minds astounds me sometimes. <br /><br />Maximilian Aue is just a byproduct of this whole history, if you can believe it. He starts out with horrific tendencies, to be sure <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: incest from an extremely young age, coprophilia, murderous inclinations <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And then comes the war and its horrible mesh of insane procedures combined with genocide in the name of a logic that only exists in minds blinded by 'the bigger picture'. The motto of the war? It's someone else's responsibility. Every bit of it. <br /><br />And the sheer idiocy of it: setting out to wipe out entire races while simultaneously saving them for an efficient work force? The entire war effort of the Germans degenerated into a paradox along these lines; at the end it became nothing more than an atrocious mess of confusion and futile attempts at maintaining order, and above all rampant killing. You look at Dr. Aue, and you look at a microcosm that contains a good deal of the horrors. The thing is, even he wasn't enough of a monster to fully appreciate them<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[; the war machine around him combined with extreme physical trauma tormented his conscience into complete insanity <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. One of the more completely fucked up characters of literature. <br /><br />You have to appreciate the detail of the book; it's so easy to sink into the world described from every aspect of cultural/political/societal context. Of course the sick taste of madness never fully leaves the pages; the aim of the book is not to leave you comfortable. Yes, quite a bit of this book will turn your stomach. But if you condemn it solely because of that, you're missing the entire point that Germany in WWII was not a nice place. It would sicken you then, so there's no point if it doesn't sicken you now.<br /><br />In a more accredited person's words:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>As long as war is regarded as wicked, it will always have its fascination. When it is looked upon as vulgar, it will cease to be popular.<pre>-Oscar Wilde</pre></i>
</blockquote> </span>
  <a data-text-id="10111672564209322196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_48730553" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48730553" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/441860-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218156664p2/441860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48730553?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/441860-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/441860-andrew?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/441860-andrew?shelf=novel-of-ideas">novel-of-ideas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48730553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7468154579971813939">Most of what has been said in criticism of this book has deeply misrepresented it: yes, there is horrible brutality well beyond simple war narration (which you will find in almost every post-WWI novel or non-fiction book). This brutality will disturb every reader (or should). Yes, some of the main character's sexual obsessions will make most readers squirm a bit even if they aren't exactly squeamish. But to read these elements as the basic character and most memorable aspects of the book is cher</span>
  <span id="freeText7468154579971813939" style="display:none">Most of what has been said in criticism of this book has deeply misrepresented it: yes, there is horrible brutality well beyond simple war narration (which you will find in almost every post-WWI novel or non-fiction book). This brutality will disturb every reader (or should). Yes, some of the main character's sexual obsessions will make most readers squirm a bit even if they aren't exactly squeamish. But to read these elements as the basic character and most memorable aspects of the book is cherry-picking--it's not a question of poor interpretation but just poor representation of the actual experience of reading this book. The representation of bureaucracy and the rather Mann-like dialogues peppering the book are, I feel, much closer to the heart of the experience of reading the novel--this is what drives the book forward and this is what really finds a home in the reader. <br /><br />I'm certainly not saying that everyone will find this book engaging, and I certainly dislike the attitude of some critics who are using this book to separate the wheat from the chaff, as it were. Asking anyone to read nearly a 1000 pages is a little presumptuous, and 1000 pages that contains maybe 75-100 extremely brutal and/or scatological pages even more so. I think the book justifies its inclusion of those 100 or so pages, and I think those 100 pages are critical to an understanding of the book, but I do not think they are, as they have been made out to be, the <i>whole</i> book, nor do they saturate the book. If you have merely been put off by the negative reviews, but were originally intending to read this book, please do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7468154579971813939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10659314569237355931">Assigning a star rating to this book is impossible. <div id="review_49516807" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49516807" />
    <a title="Denis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1729623-denis"><img alt="Denis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1227151520p2/1729623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49516807?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denis" href="/user/show/1729623-denis">Denis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49516807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8228875623246319920">I read this book in its French original version - which is important, because many American reviews are harsh with the author's writing style, and that may be due to the translation: Littell's French is actually quite beautiful - he writes in a direct, precise, elegantly classic way, and it does perfectly serve his story as well as it fits the narrator's voice. This story is, of course, at the center of the huge controversy the novel is creating - a controversy that has exploded in every country</span>
  <span id="freeText8228875623246319920" style="display:none">I read this book in its French original version - which is important, because many American reviews are harsh with the author's writing style, and that may be due to the translation: Littell's French is actually quite beautiful - he writes in a direct, precise, elegantly classic way, and it does perfectly serve his story as well as it fits the narrator's voice. This story is, of course, at the center of the huge controversy the novel is creating - a controversy that has exploded in every country where the book has been published, including in France, although the book won two of the most prestigious French literary prizes, and became a gigantic best-seller. Let's put it this way: The Kindly Ones is an extraordinary book, but it's also not without some flaws, and it is sometimes extremely difficult to read because of its subject matter. The Holocaust, and the horrors perpetrated by the Nazis, will always remain controversial material for any book of fiction - and that's understandable. Littell, though, has done the unthinkable: he's put himself into the skin of a member of the SS, who narrates his experiences through WWII - sparing us no details of the horrendous actions the Nazis did in Ukraine or in the camps, for example, but also taking us to the siege of Stalingrad, one of the darkest hour of WWII, or to the apocalyptic fall of Germany, and, more precisely, Berlin. All those pages are so vividly written, with such an incredible and indelible sense of detail, that the reader feels he's right there, where the action is taking place. Many people have been offended by those pages - mostly the ones describing the massacres of the Jews. But the thing is, those pages describe what happened, and how it happened - not with the intellectual distance of the historian, but through the eyes of a willing participant who obliges the reader to look, and to smell, and to walk among the bodies, and not to turn away. Yes, it's hell on Earth. But these events are not fiction. The horror was real - and Littell wants us to feel it in every way. His amazing talent is to write about all this as if he had been there, at the time. It's certainly very disturbing - but Littell never excuses his anti-hero, nor does he in any way condone his actions. The author's other incredible achievement is the way he describes how the Nazi machine worked during the war: the structure in power was an amazingly complex political machine, yet Littell seems to know all there is to know about it, and he takes the reader inside that world like no other writer has done it. His novel is massive: 900 pages, and it clearly hopes to be on the level of Grossman's masterpiece Life and Fate. It comes close. I read it fast. Despite the myriads of names, actions, scenes, and the intricate details of the war events that follow each other in an unstoppable flow (there are no chapters), it never is an incomprehensible book. On the contrary. Now, of course, the question is: what has Littell tried to achieve? Everybody has an opinion on the matter. I personally believe that he has tried to show us how what happened could happen, and what kind of men (sometimes intelligent, educated, sophisticated men) did what they did. What better way to do that but to let a Nazi speak? Littell's narrator believes in Nazism, is anti-semitic, participates to the holocaust: through him, we have a glimpse of how some people can justify their actions to themselves, feel a certain way about another group of human beings, and be an active part of a repressive system. As for the capacity of violence, obviously Littell thinks that everybody has it in itself: it's not a very new idea, but he demonstrates its reality with startling effects. One problem is that the main character, Aue,happens to be a very tortured man, whose past is littered with dark secrets, and who is a psychopath: I wonder if the book would not have been stronger if Aue had been a much more ordinary man. Most Germans were. When it comes to the parts of the story that are pure fiction (Aue's past, his loves, etc), Littell's is on more shaky grounds, and is not as in control of his narration as he is when he writes about the war. He's even boring and repetitive when he goes on and on about his character's sexual fantasies. The last 100 pages suffer from those flaws. But overall, The Kindly Ones is an extremely impressive achievement, which shows us the "bowels" of the Nazi party (much more than the bowels of Nazi Germany - there is a difference) like nobody else has done it before - actually, like nobody else has ever dared doing it. It is shocking, often disgusting, and you'll have nightmares. But it is an important book. I don't think, unlike some critics, that it's an unhealthy, perverse, gross, sick novel. Quite the contrary. The tour of hell it obliges us to take is a tour of humankind at its worse, and it's essential, in our days and age, to have the courage to face what supposedly civilized people can do. Yes, the events that Littell writes about are repulsive and unbelievably horrific. But he has not invented them. He has just found a way (reprehensible, according to some) to bring us as close as possible to them, and to one of the most nightmarish moment of our History, not because he wants us to take pleasure in reading about it all (and unless you're a sick person, you won't), but to make us think. And, indeed, this book made me think like few books have. It is a very unique novel. I wouldn't recommend it to everybody, for obvious reasons. But I do believe it'll remain one of the great, even if always controversial, novels of our era.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8228875623246319920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2039010182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2039010182" />
    <a title="Adam Nevill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22902860-adam-nevill"><img alt="Adam Nevill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465841872p2/22902860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2039010182?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Nevill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Nevill" href="/user/show/22902860-adam-nevill">Adam Nevill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2039010182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer788368525884241350">Finally finished Jonathan Littell's THE KINDLY ONES last night, and at 1 a.m (but needed to be in bed by 11 - often happens). Only taken me 8 years to get to this novel. It received a controversial reception at the time of publication, but I agree with the critics that hailed the novel a masterpiece, though one filled with such belief-defying horror, I often found that reading 50 pages in one sitting was enough. 1000 pages of tiny font on royal hardback pages too: took me a month to read it with</span>
  <span id="freeText788368525884241350" style="display:none">Finally finished Jonathan Littell's THE KINDLY ONES last night, and at 1 a.m (but needed to be in bed by 11 - often happens). Only taken me 8 years to get to this novel. It received a controversial reception at the time of publication, but I agree with the critics that hailed the novel a masterpiece, though one filled with such belief-defying horror, I often found that reading 50 pages in one sitting was enough. 1000 pages of tiny font on royal hardback pages too: took me a month to read it with the care and patience that the book deserves. To my taste it's a monumental work of literature, and horror in fact, much like The Road and 1984 are.<br /><br />The Kindly Ones is primarily an epic story of Dr Max Aue in WW2; a fictional autobiography of an intellectual and intelligence officer of the SS, mostly serving in the "East". It's a testament to the very worst of mankind and the worst in mankind's potential, from the murdering sadists of the Einsatzgruppe to the complicity of the ordinary. No matter how much non-fiction I've read about war, it can become more tangible and vivid and awful when the inner lives of the participants are explored by great writers of fiction.<br /><br />Uncannily, a book of our time too - the action in this story only happened 70 odd years ago, in Europe. The danger of forgetting that resonates here. So this novel can serve as recognition of how hideous we have the potential to be, in many ways, in conditions favourable to disconnecting, or repressing, reason and our humanity.<br /><br />A quote from Aue on the conflict between Germany and the USSR, in which 26.6 million died between June 1941 and May 1945. This comes at the book's beginning:<br /><br />"Thus for an overall total in my field of activities we have an average of 572,043 dead per month, 131,410 dead per week, 18,772 dead per day, 782 dead per hour, and 13.04 dead per minute of every hour of every day of every week of every month of every year of the given period, which is, as you will recall, 3 years, 10 months, 16 days, 20 hours, and 1 minute . . . If you were born in a country or at a time not only when nobody comes to kill your wife and your children, but also no one comes to ask you to kill the wives and children of others, then render thanks to God and go in peace. But always keep this thought in mind: you might be luckier than I, but you're not a better person. Because if you have the arrogance to think that you are, that's just where the danger begins. We like to contrast the State, totalitarian or not, with the ordinary man, that insect or trembling reed. But then we forget the State is made up of individuals, all more or less ordinary, each one with his life, his story, the sequence of accidents that led him one day to end up on the right side of the gun or the sheet of paper while others ended up on the wrong side. This path is rarely the result of any choice, or even of personal predilection."<br /><br />It made my head hurt, heart break, and it infected my dreams. How often does a book do that?</span>
  <a data-text-id="788368525884241350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1397205519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1397205519" />
    <a title="Lobstergirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl"><img alt="Lobstergirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380256801p2/1910836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1397205519?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lobstergirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lobstergirl" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl">Lobstergirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1910836-lobstergirl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1397205519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2768868463445915255"><br/>In some ways this was like a German <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho' title='American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis' rel='nofollow noopener'>American Psycho</a>, although that work was a pure satire, a comic novel, not historical fiction, and this (at least, I think) isn’t intended to be satire. But if you posit that both novels take a stereotypical “worst example” of each country’s upper middle class young male – a slick Wall Street misogynist on one hand and a cultivated, intelligent, ruthless SS officer on the other - and push them to the limits of sociopathy and beyond, it’s an interesting comparis</span>
  <span id="freeText2768868463445915255" style="display:none"><br />In some ways this was like a German <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho" title="American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">American Psycho</a>, although that work was a pure satire, a comic novel, not historical fiction, and this (at least, I think) isn’t intended to be satire. But if you posit that both novels take a stereotypical “worst example” of each country’s upper middle class young male – a slick Wall Street misogynist on one hand and a cultivated, intelligent, ruthless SS officer on the other - and push them to the limits of sociopathy and beyond, it’s an interesting comparison.<br /><br />Most of Littell’s novel is straight-up historical fiction, as his first person narrator Max Aue travels to Poland, Belarus, Ukraine and the Caucasus to observe the liquidation of local Jewish populations, the battle of Stalingrad, Auschwitz, late-war Berlin with Himmler, Speer and Eichmann, and the Red Army’s final westward advance. Over this he lays the disturbed personality of Aue, a man given to incest with his twin sister, matricide, vivid hallucinations, tree-fucking, and lots and lots of vomiting and diarrhea, which I took to be both visceral representations of the hell the Nazis visited upon their victims, and unconscious “sympathy” suffering mirroring the same, in the manner of a pregnant woman’s husband experiencing morning sickness. I could tell in many places where Littell and I had probably read the same historical sources, and I suspect one of those is Klaus Theweleit’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/163117.Male_Fantasies_Volume_1_Women__Floods__Bodies__History" title="Male Fantasies Volume 1 Women, Floods, Bodies, History by Klaus Theweleit" rel="nofollow noopener">Male Fantasies: Volume 1: Women, Floods, Bodies, History</a>, a book that examined the sexual and scatological perversions of the <i>Freikorps</i>.<br /><br />We see glimmers in Aue of what should not be mistaken for humanity: a concern that Jews in the death camps should be treated better and that the inmates being evacuated from Auschwitz need shoes and food is not altruism, but practicality – there’s an immense manpower shortage in Germany, and the Jews are needed for efficient slave labor. But as the novel and the war wind down, whatever mechanisms Aue has been using to keep from sliding into overt sociopathy vanish, and we see him kill not just enemies but allies, an elderly Prussian Junker who is playing Bach on a church organ, and finally <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[his best friend Thomas <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Toward the war’s end life in the Reich, even for those considered the master race, got less and less valuable. German deserters were strung up on lampposts with wild abandon, even with the manpower shortage. People were shot by their superiors for the most minuscule reasons. Littell creates a small nightmare scenario in the form of a band of Lord-of-the-Flies type boys and girls who beat Aue’s driver to death, and imitate the behavior of both the German and Russian soldiers they’ve seen, by raping the girls in their group publicly. I don’t know if this particular scene was based on historical events or fabricated, but given that it involved children, it was one of the novel’s more horrifying set-pieces.<br /><br />I’m not sure what Littell was trying to do with his novel. As storytelling, as historical fiction, there’s certainly nothing wrong with it, although I did find a sentence that went on for three and a half pages. The classic histories and biographies of the Third Reich, all together, do a better job. Obviously you don’t write a thousand page novel (if it had paragraph breaks it would probably be 1,200) with a subject which has been covered so thoroughly by the historians unless you plan to shed some new light on, explore some new aspect of, say something unique and uniquely literary about these events and these people. What enlightenment was delivered here, I can’t say.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2768868463445915255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_47472736" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47472736" />
    <a title="Jason Pettus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus"><img alt="Jason Pettus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548377232p2/147289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47472736?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Pettus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Pettus" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus">Jason Pettus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070780976" href="/book/show/241524.Les_Bienveillantes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=hipster">hipster</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/147289-jason-pettus?shelf=dark">dark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47472736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5795513128258772434">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br/><br/>Middle-aged French intellectual Jonathan Littell caused a sensation in 2006 with his infamous <i>The Kindly Ones</i> (finally published in the US for the first time in 2008), a thousand-page historical novel which attempts to take the most complicated look ever at what turned a bunch of otherwise boring, middle-</span>
  <span id="freeText5795513128258772434" style="display:none">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br /><br />Middle-aged French intellectual Jonathan Littell caused a sensation in 2006 with his infamous <i>The Kindly Ones</i> (finally published in the US for the first time in 2008), a thousand-page historical novel which attempts to take the most complicated look ever at what turned a bunch of otherwise boring, middle-class Germans into amoral monsters during the Nazi period of the 1920s through '40s (which, by the way, turns out to mostly be the same things that turned a bunch of otherwise boring, middle-class Americans into amoral monsters during the Bush years), chief among these reasons the culture of endless brutal public violence that was perpetuated in those days, said endless violence of which Littell faithfully reproduces in his own book, which is what mainly caused its infamy in the first place. But as I quickly realized when starting to make my way through it myself a few weeks ago, Littell could've actually accomplished this in a tight 300-page manuscript if he wanted; so what this doorstop mostly turns out to be instead is an insanely exhaustive examination of the jumbled bureaucracy that held Nazi Germany together, containing thousands upon thousands of offhanded references to the hundreds of sub-divisions within the party's executive structure and military setup, none of which are explained within the text itself but rather in a dense glossary at the end of the book. <i>Seriously, Littell, you're freaking killing me here.</i><br /><br />Also, Littell made the unfortunate decision to make our everyman narrator the perpetrator of a whole series of ultra-prurient sexual fetishes as well, references to which the narrator is constantly dropping into his recollection here of the war years, as casually as if he were discussing the weather (take for example his nostalgic reminisces about the solo nighttime forest wanderings he used to be able to take as a child, before mentioning that the main reason he treasures them is because he was able to indulge in his habit of auto-erotic asphyxiation without any interruptions); and this not only dilutes the book's main message (that most evil acts in history are not committed by inherently evil people, but rather normal people put into evil circumstances), but is also something I personally found profoundly offputting and completely pointless as well. The good parts of this book really are good, don't get me wrong, just that they're miniscule and surrounded by dozens of pages of needlessly disgusting or yawn-inducing fluff; and that's why I gave up on <i>The Kindly Ones</i> less than 200 pages into it, and recommend that you not even start it at all.<br /><br />Out of 10: <b>5.4</b> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5795513128258772434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review47472736">

<div id="review_2335890723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2335890723" />
    <a title="John Farebrother" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother"><img alt="John Farebrother" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499497213p2/68609816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2335890723?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Farebrother" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Farebrother" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother">John Farebrother</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070780976" href="/book/show/241524.Les_Bienveillantes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2335890723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14132408552850016987">This book is phenomenal. I worked with the author in Africa in the 1990s, and so when I first heard of this book, I was reluctant to buy it, for fear that familiarity might spoil the reading experience. When I finally did buy it, it sat on my bookshelf for several months for the same reason. But when I finally sat down and started to read it, I was hooked after a few pages.<br/>It is a journey into the soul of the SS. The author once recommended to me <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4900.Heart_of_Darkness' title='Heart of Darkness by Joseph Conrad' rel='nofollow noopener'>Heart of Darkness</a>, and I can't help thinking that</span>
  <span id="freeText14132408552850016987" style="display:none">This book is phenomenal. I worked with the author in Africa in the 1990s, and so when I first heard of this book, I was reluctant to buy it, for fear that familiarity might spoil the reading experience. When I finally did buy it, it sat on my bookshelf for several months for the same reason. But when I finally sat down and started to read it, I was hooked after a few pages.<br />It is a journey into the soul of the SS. The author once recommended to me <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4900.Heart_of_Darkness" title="Heart of Darkness by Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart of Darkness</a>, and I can't help thinking that that book among others served as a model for this work. On his Odyssey the main character encounters and participates in endless atrocities and sexual perversions, the nature of which become increasingly grotesque, but also more banal and meaningless as the war progresses. Is some of this gratuitous? Hardly. On the contrary, the baser and darker human instincts and inclinations were at the heart of Hitler's regime, and the pomp and ceremony of statehood were merely a veil concealing his true nature and that of his accomplices. The author also portrays very well the fact that the Final Solution was above all a giant bureaucracy, for the most part peopled by ordinary men and women who never killed anyone.<br />The book is also very well researched, and provides a detailed timeline of the war which forms the backdrop to the main character's narration. I followed this book with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6383424.Adolf_Hitler_The_Man_and_the_Myth" title="Adolf Hitler The Man and the Myth by Heinrich Fraenkel" rel="nofollow noopener">Adolf Hitler: The Man and the Myth</a>, which recalled to me much of the historical mentioned in this book.<br />A great read - but not for the faint hearted!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14132408552850016987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Kevin McAllister" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin McAllister" href="/user/show/233482-kevin-mcallister">Kevin McAllister</a>

  <span id="freeText14462578789716082342" style="display:none"> When the The Kindly Ones came out a few years ago in France it sold millions and won some of Europe's most prestigious literary awards . I was surprised therefore, to read so many negative reviews when it was recently released here in the U.S.<br /> It seems people either loved it or hated it. Well add me to the list of those who loved it ! Yes, its dark, depressing, and in places, deranged to the point of perversion. But that's what makes it so fascinating. And I have no doubt, that in the future, this novel will be known as one of the seminal masterpieces of modern literature.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2078692791" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2078692791" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2078692791?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070350896" href="/book/show/1693096.Les_Bienveillantes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=nazism">nazism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=world-war-2">world-war-2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2078692791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16822955342739816645">There is so much I could say about this book. First of all, it is simply impressive how much information Littell has collected on World War II and especially on the internal kitchen of Hitler Germany; I recognized a lot of what I had read in Ian Kershaw’s books (especially his Hitler biography): the ongoing internal competition between the various power centers of the Reich, the increasing anarchy, and especially the mechanism of "Dem Führer entgegen arbeiten". Of course, I cannot judge whether </span>
  <span id="freeText16822955342739816645" style="display:none">There is so much I could say about this book. First of all, it is simply impressive how much information Littell has collected on World War II and especially on the internal kitchen of Hitler Germany; I recognized a lot of what I had read in Ian Kershaw’s books (especially his Hitler biography): the ongoing internal competition between the various power centers of the Reich, the increasing anarchy, and especially the mechanism of "Dem Führer entgegen arbeiten". Of course, I cannot judge whether Littell's fictional exposition on Nazism is completely correct, but he certainly succeeded in illustrating what the internal coherence of Nazism was and how the war horrors “logically” derived from that coherence. Of course I know that this is a very controversial proposition, because many war criminals used it to try to diminish their personal responsibility.<br /><br />And that's exactly what the main character and narrator, Dr. Max Aue, a prominent member of the SS, is doing in this book. Regularly Aue states that all the main players of Nazi-Germany in fact were ordinary people like you and me, people that just wanted to play their role in a "Weltanschauung" that for them was logical and natural, and thus that they were not the sadistic demons they were represented to be after the war. Of course, since Hannah Arendt we are already aware of this, but Littell makes Aue also state quite another view: after his frequent contacts with Adolf Eichman, for example, he stresses that Eichman certainly wasn't an ordinary, banal man, but rather a top professional who only wanted to achieve the goal he had been ordered to achieve. This book is full of this kind of perverse ambiguities, constantly putting the reader on the wrong leg. All the time we have to be aware that Aue brings us an apology and thus his story is full of revisionist elements. Handsomely done, surely, but very difficult for the reader.<br /><br />Many reviews emphasize that the big weakness of this book is the lack of focus. And that's right, because Littell does not seem to be able to choose between an epic-wide evocation of a crucial era (such as Tolstoi in “War and Peace”), a philosophical-ethical reflection on the horrors of a totalitarian state (such as Vasily Grossman in “Life and Fate”), and a psychological portrait of an apparently normal but actually very sick mind (in this case, Dr. Aue). Sometimes Littell/Aue gives 100 pages of detailed information about troop movements or discussions between Nazi bosses, then suddenly a flashback from Aue on his incestuous relationship with his sister, followed by another long-eyed violent horror scene, and so on. Of course, this introspective part (because we obviously have to do with the version that Aue itself gives us) is very interesting, and Littell also explicitly connects it with the theme of the mental health of Nazism. This seems to me a key passage to understand this novel:<br /><i>"Since my childhood, I was haunted by the passion for the absolute and the transcending of limits; now this passion had led me to the edge of the mass graves of Ukraine. My thought had always been radical; now the State and the Nation also have chosen the radical and the absolute [...]. And if this radicalness was the radicalness of the abyss, and the absolute revealed itself as the absolute evil, then one ought, I firmly thought, to follow them all the way down, with the eyes wide open. “ </i><br />But, as noted, Littell does not really manage to balance this personal story of psychological perversion with the wider story of the horrors of Nazism. Instead, we get a sequence of sometimes boring, descriptive passages about the war operations, detailed descriptions of war crimes (especially the Babi Yar Massacre near Kiev, Stalingrad's Hell, the terror of Auschwitz-Birkenau and the deadly marches at the end of the war), vivid and sometimes highly philosophical conversations and occasionally also the perversions of Aue himself.<br /><br />In the last 100 pages of this book the Aue character completely derails: his acts not only become outright shocking (with some pretty graphic scenes), but sometimes also ridiculously hilarious (his encounter with Hitler for example). Perhaps Littell wanted to indicate that we should not take his story too seriously.<br /><br />No, this novel, although at times very impressive, certainly is not an overall success. Personally, I think that 1400 pages (in my edition) really is too much. If you really want to read an extended book about war and dictatorship, I would certainly recommend the 1.000 pages of Vasily Grossman's "Life and Fate". (2.5 stars)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16822955342739816645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111491869" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11198371334361861951">This may not be evident, but I tend to avoid historical fiction set during World War II. I'm not sure why: it's an obvious (perhaps too obvious) source of material for exploring the human condition. I'm not squeamish about the details of the Holocaust. Maybe it's just that a lot of World War II fiction focuses on the battles, the military strategy and tactics, and it's <em>military fiction</em> that I'm avoiding. In the last month, however, I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40847438' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <em>Time's Arrow</em>
</a> and <em>The Kindly Ones</em>, both of which are m</span>
  <span id="freeText11198371334361861951" style="display:none">This may not be evident, but I tend to avoid historical fiction set during World War II. I'm not sure why: it's an obvious (perhaps too obvious) source of material for exploring the human condition. I'm not squeamish about the details of the Holocaust. Maybe it's just that a lot of World War II fiction focuses on the battles, the military strategy and tactics, and it's <em>military fiction</em> that I'm avoiding. In the last month, however, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40847438" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>Time's Arrow</em>
</a> and <em>The Kindly Ones</em>, both of which are memoirs to an extent; now I'm reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111491794" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>A Thread of Grace</em>
</a>. Compared to Martin Amis, Jonathan Littell gets at least one thing right: he tells the story in roughly chronological order. And you know what, Amis? <em>It works better that way.</em> Unfortunately, <em>The Kindly Ones</em> eludes the masterpiece status being accorded to it by some reviewers. Maybe it deserves more credit than I'm willing to give it, but some of the critical acclaim it has received is just . . . silly.<br><br>The back cover of my edition has a blurb from <em>Kirkus Reviews</em>: "Littell's apocalyptic ending is like nothing else in the literature. . . . The closing is a tour de force. But so is the entire book . . . with not a wasted word." That is serious hyperbole for any book, and it's outright preposterous for this ponderous 983-page novel. A book this thick does <em>not</em> get the benefit of a doubt. And the page count is only the beginning, for <em>The Kindly Ones</em> shelters monstrous multi-page paragraphs—in fact, I think no paragraph is under one page in length, and I counted one as long as seven pages. If the scope of this novel is broad enough to stretch from occupied France to the Russian front, the level of detail dives down toward the infinitesimal. This book is a prime example of why I have a "literary pretentious" shelf at all.<br><br>Of course, pretentiousness and ponderousness are not ipso facto <em>bad</em>. Still, there is a reason that all fiction must elide certain parts of the narrative; it's simply impractical to include every detail about every event from the beginning to the end of the story. An author chisels a good narrative from a block of wild, untamed story, wrapping it in perspectives and limitations until we can comprehend the tale. The skill requires knowing which parts to remove as well as which parts to keep (and a good editor helps too). As with any rule of writing, it's permissible to bend or break this one. But I'm sceptical <em>The Kindly Ones</em> earns such permission. It's obvious that Littell has <a href="http://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Main/ShownTheirWork" rel="nofollow noopener">done his research</a>, and this results in an immersive depiction of Nazi Europe I have not often encountered. Nevertheless, for every harrowing description of the treatment of prisoners or the twisted veneer of civilization in Berlin, there is a passage submerged in a quagmire of impenetrable military jargon from which the glossary at the end provides no relief. Reading this book is a little bit like going to war against it.<br><br>Though the book itself may sometimes forget this fact, <em>The Kindly Ones</em> is a memoir. Maximilien Aue is an officer in the SS, but he is not a soldier, not in the classical, warrior sense. He is a bureaucrat, a legal scholar—an intellectual, which is always an interesting and controversial word. There is plenty of violence, death, and bloodshed, but this is not a book about the battles or the strategy. Aue himself, when quizzed on these subjects, reminds people that strategy isn't his department: he's a paper-pusher who, for some reason, often ends up too close to the firing line, whether literal or metaphorical. Most importantly, Aue offers us—or seems to offer us—the perspective of a Nazi soldier who does <em>not</em> unreservedly hate all Jews and want to wipe them from the face of the Earth. Yes, he works toward this goal, but only because it's a logistical necessity toward winning the war—or so he claims. Whether or not Aue's apathy toward anti-Semitism is genuine, Littell does manage to create a complex picture of German attitudes toward the Holocaust. He emphasizes that the Nazi treatment of Jews extended to other demographics as well. Most importantly, amid the rabid and stereotypical Jew-hating Nazis he includes soldiers and citizens who, far from hating Jews, regret and lament the Führer's Final Solution. In a way, these people's existence is even more terrifying than the existence of villains like Hitler and Himmler—they are a reminder that supposedly rational and reasonable individuals recognized how <em>wrong</em> the Holocaust was and still did nothing about it. Sure, we can demonize every SS man and claim that they were all pure evil. Yet that, in my opinion, diminishes the sincerity with which we remember the Holocaust. It is a manufacturing of an Other where there is none, an attempt to distance ourselves from those who committed these atrocities. Rightly horrified by the Holocaust, we declare, "Only Others could have done this, only monsters," because it reassures us that we do not possess any such darkness within ourselves.<br><br>This conceit Aue seeks to refute. Almost immediately he explains that, regardless of his flaws (and there are oh so many) he is still a human being:<br><blockquote>But I don't think I'm a devil. There were always reasons for what I did. Good reasons or bad reasons, I don't know, in any case human reasons. Those who kill are humans, just like those who are killed, that's what's terrible.</blockquote><br>The tragedy is not that extremist bigots exist but that ordinary people will, given the proper motivation, follow those bigots and participate in disasters like the Holocaust. War is terrible precisely because it is <em>not</em> a natural disaster, an act of God that we can neither prevent nor mitigate. War is a human action, an evil perpetrated on humans <em>by</em> humans. And few humans can ever say that they will never kill, regardless of the circumstance:<br><blockquote>You can never say: I shall never kill, that's impossible; the most you can say is: I hope I shall never kill. I too hoped so, I too wanted to live a good and useful life, to be a man among men, equal to others, I too wanted to add my brick to our common house. But my hopes were dashed, and my sincerity was betrayed and placed at the services of an ultimately evil and corrupt work, and I <em>crossed over to the dark shores</em>, and all this evil entered my own life, and none of this can be made whole, ever.</blockquote><br>This passage affected me the most. It's a reminder of the uncomfortable truth of my own humanity. I'm not saying I'd sign up for the SS and start marching and saluting the Führer. But maybe I wouldn't be brave enough to stand against it. And certainly, given the right circumstances, I could be driven to kill.<br><br>So Littell reaches into the core of my being . . . on page 24, barely 2 per cent of the way through this epic. Yes, everything after this point is downhill (although not evenly so; there are various crests and troughs along the way). There are several reasons for this decline, but one in particular relates to Littell's theme of ordinary people participating in extraordinary atrocities. Aue insists that he is "a man like other men, I am a man like you. I tell you I am just like you!" Except he <em>isn't</em>. At the extreme end, Littell has decided to rehash the <em>Oresteia</em> with Nazis. Aue is a psychopath. He murders his mother and stepfather, and later, several more people in cold blood. I'm willing to attribute these over-the-top actions to the bullet he takes through the brain while in Stalingrad. Still, he was having fantasies about his sister long before he joined the SS; they just intensify as the war goes on and Aue becomes increasingly unhinged and unpredictable. I don't begrudge Littell his Greek allusions or Aue's psychological baggage—but they do bely any claim that Aue is somehow "like us."<br><br>Honestly, though, I didn't get the point of Aue's baggage. If we want to get Freudian, we're supposed to believe it all stems from animosity toward a harsh, and later absentee, father. He resents his mother for not breastfeeding him (he was allergic to her milk). And his frustrated, unrequited desire for his sister has fouled any notion of heterosexual intimacy; rather, he seeks sodomy with other men in an attempt to feel closer to his sister through the act of penetration. But what's the point of the hallucinatory, unreliable flashbacks and memories that Aue recounts to us? Is this Littell's way of justifying Aue's solitary nature (why does this need to be justified)? Or does Littell include this out of the sheer disturbing fascination it provokes? This part of the story culminates in Aue's complete surrender to his fantasies while occupying his sister's empty house in Pomerania. Aue has spent the last week wandering his sister's house while naked, masturbating in the woods, and experiencing . . . scatological perturbations. Thomas, arguably his only friend, comes to retrieve him. The Russians have advanced almost past this point, and their return to Berlin becomes a flight from the encroaching enemy. But by this point in the novel, I'm skimming, and I've lost any sense of sympathy or empathy for Aue.<br><br>If Maximilien Aue were the only thing <em>The Kindly Ones</em> had to offer, it would be a sorry excuse for a novel. Fortunately, Littell's prose supplies solutions as well as problems. Aue shows us aspects of the war that, if not undocumented in fiction, certainly escape the history books. In school, I learned about World War II as a series of episodic battles, culminating in V-E and V-J days. Perhaps necessarily simplistic, this presentation ignores the quotidian operations behind the front. The two most captivating parts of this slow-paced story were Stalingrad and the latter half of Berlin. In both cases, Aue was involved in administrative work. Normally that would be dull, but in this case it provides a perspective on the war effort to which I am unaccustomed.<br><br>Also, there are some serious ruminations on ideology and methodology in this book. Aue has a nice conversation with a Communist prisoner, who compares National Socialism to Communism: both are deterministic and assert the existence of an objective enemy. These philosophical digressions are a welcome interlude from the jargon-laden descriptions of military activity. Now if only the dialogue was separated by paragraphs . . . ugh.<br><br>And while Aue did not impress me, and Thomas was very transparently opportunistic, there were two characters who found a place in my heart. Both met untimely ends. Voss, a linguist who befriends Aue, is the least evil Nazi I've ever seen. He's a specialist in Indo-Germanic languages, but <em>he's</em> using the war as an excuse to plunder rare texts from the libraries of occupied countries. (OK, this might be an evil act, but when you're up against "killing all the Jews," even the destruction of cultural heritage pales in comparison.) Piontek, Aue's driver, is loyal and competent. He reminds me a little of another character, Ivan, who protects Aue while he's in Stalingrad. Piontek is a grunt, and thus doesn't have much personality of his own, but along with Thomas and Helene he is one of the few people with whom Aue has any sort of healthy comradeship. The emphasis there is on "healthy." Aue's experiences during wartime are, for the most part, distinctly unhealthy. And he's right to blame the war—after all, what <em>is</em> it good for? Yet the war can't be entirely to blame, and Aue never does seem contrite, just crazy.<br><br><em>The Kindly Ones</em> consists of many serious, worthwhile ideas suspended in a robust but tasteless broth of prose. Is it too long? Well, it does itself no favours by being this length, and a shorter book would probably earn more leniency (I'm not saying the system is fair). To condemn it on length alone is a shallow assessment, though. <em>The Kindly Ones</em> isn't just overly long; it's <em>messy</em>, so merely cutting out material isn't going to remedy much. There are fascinating and moving parts to this book, enough so that it's not a total loss. But I can't be much more enthusiastic than that, nor can I really recommend it to others. And that's where the length becomes the true issue: pages are investments, and for all of Littell's obvious research and effort, <em>The Kindly Ones</em> is an investment that provides little return.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11198371334361861951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
